    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({

<span id="freeTextContainer15444862738316064210">Throughout history, some books have changed the world. They have transformed the way we see ourselves—and each other.   <span id="freeText15444862738316064210" style="display:none">Throughout history, some books have changed the world. They have transformed the way we see ourselves—and each other. They have inspired debate, dissent, war and revolution. They have enlightened, outraged, provoked and comforted. They have enriched lives—and destroyed them. Now Penguin brings you the works of the great thinkers, pioneers, radicals and visionaries whose ideas shook civilization and helped make us who we are. Inspired by the myth of a man condemned to ceaselessly push a rock up a mountain and watch it roll back to the valley below, <i>The Myth of Sisyphus</i> transformed twentieth-century philosophy with its impassioned argument for the value of life in a world without religious meaning.</span>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/614778-ahmad-sharabiani?shelf=non-fiction">non-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/614778-ahmad-sharabiani?shelf=legends">legends</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/614778-ahmad-sharabiani?shelf=literature">literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/614778-ahmad-sharabiani?shelf=20th-century">20th-century</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer50373274" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer597352752580237707">Le Mythe de Sisyphe = The myth of Sisyphus and other essays, Albert Camus<br/><br/>The Myth of Sisyphus is a 1942 philosophical essay by Albert Camus. <br/><br/>In the last chapter, Camus outlines the legend of Sisyphus who defied the gods and put Death in chains so that no human needed to die. <br/><br/>When Death was eventually liberated and it came time for Sisyphus himself to die, he concocted a deceit which let him escape from the underworld. <br/><br/>After finally capturing Sisyphus, the gods decided that his punishment would l</span>
  <span id="freeText597352752580237707" style="display:none">Le Mythe de Sisyphe = The myth of Sisyphus and other essays, Albert Camus<br /><br />The Myth of Sisyphus is a 1942 philosophical essay by Albert Camus. <br /><br />In the last chapter, Camus outlines the legend of Sisyphus who defied the gods and put Death in chains so that no human needed to die. <br /><br />When Death was eventually liberated and it came time for Sisyphus himself to die, he concocted a deceit which let him escape from the underworld. <br /><br />After finally capturing Sisyphus, the gods decided that his punishment would last for all eternity. He would have to push a rock up a mountain; upon reaching the top, the rock would roll down again, leaving Sisyphus to start over. <br /><br />Camus sees Sisyphus as the absurd hero who lives life to the fullest, hates death, and is condemned to a meaningless task.<br /><br />تاریخ نخستین خوانش: ماه مارس سال 1972میلادی<br /><br />عنوان: افسانه سیزیف؛ آلبر کامو؛ مترجمها خامها و آقایان: «علی صدوقی»؛ «محمدعلی سپانلو»؛ «اکبر افسری»؛ «محمدصادق رئیسی»؛ «محمود سلطانیه»؛ «افسانه نجومی»؛ و «مهستی بحرینی»؛ «شهلا شریعتمداری»؛<br /><br />نخستین بار مطبوعاتی فرخی در سال 1342هجری خورشیدی در 154ص، با ترجمه ی جنابان آقایان «محمدعلی سپانلو» و «علی صدوقی»؛<br />مترجم: شهلا شریعتمداری، تهران، ناشر: نامعلوم، 1350، در 174ص<br />مترجم: محمود سلطانیه؛ تهران، جامی، 1384؛ در 160ص؛ چاپ پنجم 1392؛<br />مترجم: مهستی بحرینی، تهران، نیلوفر؛ 1393، در 175ص<br />مترجم: محمدصادق رئیسی؛ تهران، روزگار نو، 1398، در 156ص؛<br /><br />خدایان، «سیزیف» را محکوم کرده بودند، که دائما سنگی را به بالای کوهی بغلطاند، تا جاییکه سنگ بخاطر وزنش فرو افتد؛ آنها فکر میکردند تنبیهی وحشتناکتر از انجام کاری عبث و بیهوده، وجود ندارد؛ اما به گفته ی «هومر»، «سیزیف» خردمندترین و محتاط ترین موجود فانی بود؛ باز به گفته ی «هومر»، «سیزیف» مرگ را در زنجیر کرده بود، و این فرمانروایی، خدایان را خوش نمیآمد؛ پس «پلاتو»، خدای جنگ را فرستاد، تا مرگ را از دستان اشغالگر «سیزیف» آزاد سازد؛ ...؛<br /><br />گروهی «اسطوره سیزیف» را، یکی از مهم‌ترین آثار فلسفی «آلبر کامو» می‌دانند؛ به باور «کامو»، خودکشی جدی‌ترین مسئله‌ ی فلسفه است، و در واقع پاسخ به همین پرسش است که آیا زندگی ارزش زیستن را دارد؟ زندگی و خودکشی، درون‌مایه ‌ی بخشی از آثار «کامو» هستند که با عنوان «سه‌ گانه ‌ی پوچي» شناخته می‌شوند، کتابهای «بیگانه» یک رمان، «کالیگولا» در قالب نمایشنامه، و «اسطوره سیزیف» یک اثر فلسفی، که هر سه کتاب نشان ‌دهنده‌ی باورهای «کامو» درباره‌ ی «پوچی» هستند؛<br /><br />تاریخ بهنگام رسانی 04/06/199هجری خورشیدی؛ ا. شربیانی</span>
  <a data-text-id="597352752580237707" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_23140030" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/23140030" />
    <a title="Erik Graff" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/974210-erik-graff"><img alt="Erik Graff" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1299888977p2/974210.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/23140030?book_show_action=true">May 28, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Erik Graff" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Erik Graff" href="/user/show/974210-erik-graff">Erik Graff</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/10062440-the-myth-of-sisyphus">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">troubled teens</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Erik by: </span>
      no one
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/974210-erik-graff?shelf=philosophy">philosophy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer23140030" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3751429010301180383">By the end of high school I was a very unhappy person and had been so since our family moved from unincorporated Kane County to Park Ridge, Illinois when I was ten. At the outset the unhappiness was basically consequent upon leaving a rural setting, small school and friendly, integrated working-class neighborhood for a reactionary suburb, large school and unfriendly upper middle-class populace whose children were, by and large, just as thoughtlessly racist and conservative as their parents were.</span>
  <span id="freeText3751429010301180383" style="display:none">By the end of high school I was a very unhappy person and had been so since our family moved from unincorporated Kane County to Park Ridge, Illinois when I was ten. At the outset the unhappiness was basically consequent upon leaving a rural setting, small school and friendly, integrated working-class neighborhood for a reactionary suburb, large school and unfriendly upper middle-class populace whose children were, by and large, just as thoughtlessly racist and conservative as their parents were. By fifteen, however, the quality of the unhappiness had begun to change as I had made, really made, some friends in the persons of Richard Hyde and Hank Kupjack. By the end of high school, thanks to them and to the rise of the sixties counterculture, I actually had many friends, some of them from the political left, some identified with the avant garde world, some just plain disgruntled teen potheads. But by then unhappiness had become character and had been elevated from an emotional to a philosophical state of being.<br /><br />On the one hand, it had a lot to do with not having had a girlfriend since Lisa in the first grade. On the other hand, and this was more prominently to mind, it had to do with the reasons, the serious reasons, for not having one. They were that I was unusually slow in physical development and unusually short in stature. In my mind, I was uncontestably unattractive. If any girl would like me it would be because of personality and intelligence,<br /><br />I had no insecurity about intelligence as a teen, but quite a bit about personality. Feminism didn't become an issue until college, but I was ashamed about thinking of women sexually when it seemed clear they would be offended or disgusted were they to know of it. I developed the practice of not looking at females unless speaking with them. I walked with my head down, eyes to the ground, in order to avoid such guilt-ridden gazes. While other guys played around with the girls in our circle, I maintained a generally grave persona, holding "serious" conversations or reading while they flirted. A feeling of superiority was confusedly mixed with strong feelings of inferiority to these other, more comfortable, persons. While it was easy to dismiss most of the "straight" kids at school as mindless, this was not possible with many persons in our circle, particularly some of the older ones whom I admired for their learning and critical intellects.<br /><br />The other, philosophically deeper, dimension of this unease was that I myself was so "critically intelligent" that I had no ground upon which to stand. I had strong moral feelings but I was unable to convince myself that they were more than personal tastes. My early public school education had emphasized the sciences. While I could understand human values as having some meaning in terms of biology and evolutionary theory, I could not fit myself positively into that picture. I certainly wasn't biologically "fit". Thoughts of suicide were frequent.<br /><br />Thus I was drawn, upon being exposed to them, to the existentialists, particularly Camus. They alone seemed to be trying to speak openly about the actual human condition<br /><br />I recall reading "The Myth of Sisyphus" while seated in our family's red Opel Cadet station wagon across from City Hall, at the curb of Hodge's Park on a beautiful spring day. Our friends were all about this area between Bob Rowe's Evening Pipe Shop, Park Ridge's Community Church and the Cogswell Dance Studio (our indoors hangouts), but I was avoiding their frivolity, engaged in serious study, while, obviously, inviting an invitation to join in--which, in my moral confusion, I might well have declined.<br /><br />Just as I was concluding this essay of the collection, the part about Sisyphus being happy with his absurd work, Lisa Cox walked in front of the car, headed west towards the church. Now, Lisa was just another pretty girl in our group, not the particular object of any attention from me. Indeed, she was too young, being two years behind in school. But, not being an intimate friend, she was one of those girls I would tend to guiltily objectify as sexual.<br /><br />Here, however, it happened differently. She was beautiful, simply beautiful. Her long, tightly waved brown hair and matching corduroy pants, all bathed in sunlight dappled by the new leaves of the elms filling the park, were lovely. I didn't feel guilty for thinking this. I noticed the absence of guilt feelings. It seemed quite paradoxical, just as Camus' comment about Sisyphus had appeared, but true.<br /><br />I'd call this an ecstatic experience. It didn't last more than a few minutes at most, though the memory of it, and experiences like it, remains clear and cherished.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3751429010301180383" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3050589791" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3050589791" />
    <a title="Fergus" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/70395042-fergus"><img alt="Fergus" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1567548184p2/70395042.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3050589791?book_show_action=true">Jan 16, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Fergus" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Fergus" href="/user/show/70395042-fergus">Fergus</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/25857411-the-myth-of-sisyphus">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3050589791" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5774088620349067341">When I picked up this beloved old book this morning, after awakening from a painfully fitful sleep, the words in it seemed to be my own. <br/><br/>They are all that clearly familiar to me, after so many years away from them.<br/><br/>So it goes with life. <br/><br/>As we approach the years of our old age, the routine of our life falls into place without our even trying - if we have been paying attention to it.<br/><br/>That’s because the way we now live our life is something obvious, like the habits of a dear old friend. There are few</span>
  <span id="freeText5774088620349067341" style="display:none">When I picked up this beloved old book this morning, after awakening from a painfully fitful sleep, the words in it seemed to be my own. <br /><br />They are all that clearly familiar to me, after so many years away from them.<br /><br />So it goes with life. <br /><br />As we approach the years of our old age, the routine of our life falls into place without our even trying - if we have been paying attention to it.<br /><br />That’s because the way we now live our life is something obvious, like the habits of a dear old friend. There are few surprises. Things are lucid.<br /><br />We have become, as Auden says so well, like the etched strata of a limestone cliff - for we have become our Faults, friendly qualities with which we are now as familiar as with the back of our hand.<br /><br />So it is with that apparition which Camus here calls the worm in our heart. For that is the very heart of the evil in our world.<br /><br />The worm in the heart is self-interest. It suspends our disbelief in our personal stories. We start believing in a self who has continuity and is progressing over time. Towards what?<br /><br />Nothing, really - but our pleasure in our life stories persuade us that they’re true. But Camus is saying that to see the truth, we have to come to grips with emptiness and face the end of our stories. <br /><br />We have to wake up to a life emptied of frills and diversions. That is his counter-attack on the worm’s self-aggrandizing illusions.<br /><br />Others have undertaken that same attack on their egos. Like in Ryan Holiday’s The Ego is the Enemy.<br /><br />And I think of Bach, and his dour middle period of penitential music. I believe he successfully eliminated his Daemon of pride in his Pietist practises, as was reflected in this beautiful, mournful music.<br /><br />How we choose the inevitable flight from that too-lucid apparition will decide our destiny. After that, our habits become something we can modify.<br /><br />When I was a very young teen in the throes of coming of age, I - in my fear - chose the framework of a Christian mindset with which to judge my urges, and I’m glad I did. It has served me very ably. <br /><br />Unfortunately, my young mind was too predisposed to dreaming, to interpret this mindset as anything other than mystical and dream-like.<br /><br />As Gérard de Nerval sang so well:<br /><br />J’ai deux fois vainqueur traversé l’Acheron <br />Modulant tour à tour sur la lyre d’Orphee <br />Les soupirs de la Sainte et les cris de la fée.<br /><br />In fact, it is the polar opposite of the dreamily affective, this conscious wide-awake awareness: for it’s intensely practical.<br /><br />It is the very beginning of an annulment of emotional involvement in our stories, eventually resulting in a more natural and real love. <br /><br />My sudden realization that I had always had a condition known as Asperger’s syndrome helped enormously here.<br /><br />I can verify that fact now, in light of the habitual ease of my generally virtuous habits being slightly autistic in nature - however jarringly at odds with reality they may seem to my contemporaries.<br /><br />My insight, and my meds, trimmed the accumulated fat from that goodness, thank heaven! And the love of my wife and friends helped a lot too.<br /><br />All well and good so far. But there’s a problem here.<br /><br />For though the Framework of my thoughts was useful and viable, my habitual responses to that worm in the heart had not been that.<br /><br />I always chose A Dark FLIGHT from that Worm - Camus says we all do - when I could have chosen a Lucid Stand to be Perfectly Conscious of it. Avoidance is built into our modern way of life.<br /><br />For if we answer the Lucidity of the Worm with the Lucidity of Conscious Awareness, we will still, like the rest of the human race, veer in our unguarded moments towards weakness and disaster.<br /><br />But here’s the thing: by lucid awareness of the worm’s nonbeing we can make the whole scenario transparent to our own habitual subconscious thinking.<br /><br />As Camus does by making the monsters of nothingness dissolve.<br /><br />And what happens when the Worm is seen through?<br /><br />Our life gains a New Quality: Peace.<br /><br />THAT is what happens when, as Eliot says, “the Kingfisher’s wing answers Light to Light, and is Silent.”<br /><br />Did you get that? <br /><br />The King shines the Light of Heaven on our lucid struggle with a Very Lucid Enemy.<br /><br />And His Silence thereafter is our Peace...<br /><br />And a Sign of His Blessing:<br /><br />For, as the psalmist says, “Ce goût du néant est (seulement) le goût du mensonge!”<br /><br />And That’s how our old age can become transparent -<br /><br />With a sense of humdrum tranquility.<br /><br />And a return to daytime normalcy after the midnight nightmares of the worm.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5774088620349067341" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/56939723-samra-yusuf?shelf=nobel-writers">nobel-writers</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2247089506" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16317833649781071879">No matter in what farthest corner of the world you live, which color is of your skin, what kind of habits you’ve grown over the time for you to be known as a busy person, what are the erogenous fantasies your mind weave in the moments of quiet to make you tremble with pleasure, which, from many doctrines you chose to scale the things as “right” and “wrong” which one from countless delusions you’ve opted as religion, or you weren’t the one to opt it, you inherited it like other concrete property,</span>
  <span id="freeText16317833649781071879" style="display:none">No matter in what farthest corner of the world you live, which color is of your skin, what kind of habits you’ve grown over the time for you to be known as a busy person, what are the erogenous fantasies your mind weave in the moments of quiet to make you tremble with pleasure, which, from many doctrines you chose to scale the things as “right” and “wrong” which one from countless delusions you’ve opted as religion, or you weren’t the one to opt it, you inherited it like other concrete property, to which fairy god you sold your reason in exchange of a fabricated assurance of hereafter and a hoax of a succor for your inner void, it is absurd to find meaning in the meaninglessness of life, to keep asking questions for which there’s no answer, because life doesn’t offer any, there’s nothing like “truth” in this senseless world Camus puts it as: “That universal reason, practical or ethical, that determinism, those categories that explain everything are enough to make a decent man laugh” (MS, 21).<br />So, one is inclined to ask, is life worth living? If not, why don’t we cease to exist, as there’s no meaning to keep going on a path which leads to nowhere but right at the point we started our journey from, if there’s no hope of life after this one, why to live this one to begin with, and this leads us to ‘existential anxiety’, for Camus, it is only when one abandons hope, one can live to one’s fullest, 'Abandon hope all ye who enter here' and live because we are our fate, and our frustration is our very life: we can never escape it, and consequently, the truly one philosophical question “suicide” must be out of question, simply not an option, because if life’s not worth living for someone who strives to have a meaning, so is death, there’s no point in committing suicide, because it entails that one is quitting to something he couldn’t grasp, let we be indifferent to what that simply doesn’t make a difference.<br />So did our absurd hero, Sisyphus, who was punished by gods for airing secrecy, he was to lift a boulder heaviest than skies on his shoulders, and climb the mountain, by reaching up, the boulder will roll down with Godspeed and Sisyphus had to watch it all the while, lift the boulder, ascent the mountain, watch it rolling down, for eternity. But the pleasure lies in knowing, Sisyphus knew the meaninglessness of his act, the absurdity of doing it again and again, with no hope in way, with more passion every time he goes down to lift the boulder, with new intensity, never resigning himself to despair, because despair roots out from presence of hope, if there’s no hope otherwise, certainly never is there despair. And for Camus, Sisyphus' triumph is his act of this absurdity “His scorn of the gods, his hatred of death, and his passion for life won him that unspeakable penalty in which the whole being is exerted toward accomplishing nothing” (MS, 120)..</span>
  <a data-text-id="16317833649781071879" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1871018341" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1871018341" />
    <a title="BlackOxford" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/17744555-blackoxford"><img alt="BlackOxford" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1484863606p2/17744555.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1871018341?book_show_action=true">Jan 08, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="BlackOxford" class="user" itemprop="url" name="BlackOxford" href="/user/show/17744555-blackoxford">BlackOxford</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17744555-blackoxford?shelf=french-language">french-language</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17744555-blackoxford?shelf=philosophy-theology">philosophy-theology</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1871018341" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11519668357331991272"><b>Assisted Living</b><br/><br/>It was that Jewish heretic Paul of Tarsus who gave us the idea that we are not in charge of our lives but are merely responsible for them to God who owns us. It was the English philosopher John Locke, a heretic to Pauline Calvinism, who casually pointed out that in fact our lives are the only thing we do have complete charge over, the only thing every one of us owns and can dispose of. And it was Albert Camus, a heretic to any and all sources of power, who took Locke entirely seri</span>
  <span id="freeText11519668357331991272" style="display:none"><b>Assisted Living</b><br /><br />It was that Jewish heretic Paul of Tarsus who gave us the idea that we are not in charge of our lives but are merely responsible for them to God who owns us. It was the English philosopher John Locke, a heretic to Pauline Calvinism, who casually pointed out that in fact our lives are the only thing we do have complete charge over, the only thing every one of us owns and can dispose of. And it was Albert Camus, a heretic to any and all sources of power, who took Locke entirely seriously by pointing out that how we dispose of life is the central issue of not just life but philosophy. The result is Sisyphus.<br /><br />The followers of previous heretics - evangelical Christians, PC and wet liberals - don't like Camus. But they can't fault his conclusions. They may not approve of his marketing of suicide as a universally available option for disposing of life, but these are the same people who don't approve of gay sex or the discussion of religion in public. So hardly credible. Clearly Camus's analysis includes both Paul's and Locke's as special cases, and is therefore superior to them both. <br /><br />Camus doesn't advocate suicide; he does advocate its importance to life and thought. Without it we are dead, as it were, all but physically. Habit and chance rule. Life is not inherently absurd but becomes so when death, specifically self-inflicted death, is not on the table. Evasions - illusion, after-life, hope, consuming, power, sex, reputation - become the norm that is socially enforced. Eliminating evasions is what Camus is trying to do.<br /><br />There is rarely a page in Sisyphus without a phrase to savour and as memorable as anything in Montaigne. Just for openers:<br /><br />p2: "I have never seen anyone die for the ontological argument."<br /><br />p3: "Beginning to think is beginning to be undermined."<br /><br />p4: "A world that can be explained even with bad reasons is a familiar world."<br /><br />So even if the logic gets you down, you have some rather sustaining prose to exchange with the spouse or functional equivalent over breakfast.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11519668357331991272" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_680338412" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/680338412" />
    <a title="Zanna" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/22591633-zanna"><img alt="Zanna" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1512813535p2/22591633.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/680338412?book_show_action=true">Jul 29, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Zanna" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Zanna" href="/user/show/22591633-zanna">Zanna</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780141023991" href="/book/show/612957.The_Myth_of_Sisyphus">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22591633-zanna?shelf=philosophy">philosophy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer680338412" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5672625611691458564">A good friend introduced me to Nietzsche in my early teens, and Nietzsche and I have had a turbulent relationship ever since. One of the first adult books I read was Kafka's <i>The Trial</i> and Nietzsche was there too, inviting me to step off the city on poles into the bottomless swamp.<br/><br/><a href='https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=dFJAol9pgxM' rel='nofollow noopener'>
  <i>Oh baby hold my hand<br/>we're gonna walk on water</i>
</a><br/><br/>Nietzsche said there are no facts, no truth. After he said this, some philosophers stopped writing like Kant and wrote like poets. Camus says here that 'there is no truth</span>
  <span id="freeText5672625611691458564" style="display:none">A good friend introduced me to Nietzsche in my early teens, and Nietzsche and I have had a turbulent relationship ever since. One of the first adult books I read was Kafka's <i>The Trial</i> and Nietzsche was there too, inviting me to step off the city on poles into the bottomless swamp.<br /><br /><a href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=dFJAol9pgxM" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <i>Oh baby hold my hand<br />we're gonna walk on water</i>
</a><br /><br />Nietzsche said there are no facts, no truth. After he said this, some philosophers stopped writing like Kant and wrote like poets. Camus says here that 'there is no truth, merely truths. From the evening breeze to this hand on my shoulder.' Consciousness creates a 'shimmer' of truths.<br /><br />If there is no god and we are all condemned to death and I am conscious then my life is <i>absurd</i>. Existentialists arrived here and made their leaps of faith to gods. Karl Popper made the leap of faith to reason (reason is Popper's god. There is no <i>a priori</i> argument for reason), but Camus does not want to leap, he asks if we can live with what we <i>have</i>, this absurd life, and not kill ourselves. Nietzsche said we make art in order not to commit suicide. Camus tells us that Dostoyevsky found his 'leap' here - if we cannot bear to live without belief in an immortal soul, then the immortal soul must be!<br /><br />Camus will not leap and he will not choose suicide: he decides we can live with what we have if we remain lucid and conscious and don't succumb to illusion. After Camus, some artists created in order to provoke and maintain the absurd consciousness. This is the effect Beckett gets, I think, in Waiting for Godot. The sleeplessness, the watchfulness, the silliness of Camus' absurdity.<br /><br />I have myself been tempted by the leap, to reason or the immortal soul. But in the main I have lived after Nietzsche, without much anguish. I do not find it so hard to 'imagine Sisyphus happy', to watch with Camus as he walks down to the valley of hell after his rock to start over, stronger than the rock then, striding unencumbered. I've been busy and the birds have sung and food has tasted good and love has touched me. (These White men who had so little to do that they were overwhelmed by grief for lost illusions might have felt better after baking a loaf or sweeping out the house. In all seriousness.)<br /><br />Camus gives three sketches of 'absurd men'. Don Juan and the conqueror I have no use for, as with much of this book, I discard them as too mired in patriarchy to use without starting again. But the sketch of the actor sings out to me. <blockquote>'What matters,' said Nietzsche, 'is not eternal life but eternal vivacity.' All drama is, in fact, in this choice.</blockquote> Not because we should live as though in the limelight, or even because there is no rehearsal (no eternal return) but because in drama the shimmer of truth is shared. Camus does not seem to have thought of this: his absurd man is oh tragically alone (again I advise him: bath the baby, wash the linen). But in the theatre we are not alone, we are fish in the water of each other's truths, we can live them in these mirrors. As another philosopher said, <a href="http://roseannastar.wordpress.com/2013/09/01/one-person-is-a-fiction-2/" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <i>there has never been one person</i>
</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="5672625611691458564" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_150454560" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/150454560" />
    <a title="Ian &quot;Marvin&quot; Graye" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5022264-ian-marvin-graye"><img alt="Ian &quot;Marvin&quot; Graye" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1594424898p2/5022264.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/150454560?book_show_action=true">Feb 24, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ian &quot;Marvin&quot; Graye" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ian &quot;Marvin&quot; Graye" href="/user/show/5022264-ian-marvin-graye">Ian &quot;Marvin&quot; Graye</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/24383287-the-myth-of-sisyphus">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Anybody who prefers Rolling Stones to Beatles albums</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5022264-ian-marvin-graye?shelf=cul-poli-phil-art">cul-poli-phil-art</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5022264-ian-marvin-graye?shelf=camus">camus</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5022264-ian-marvin-graye?shelf=re-read">re-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5022264-ian-marvin-graye?shelf=read-2015">read-2015</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5022264-ian-marvin-graye?shelf=reviews">reviews</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5022264-ian-marvin-graye?shelf=reviews-4-stars">reviews-4-stars</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer150454560" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2045981194290321012"><b>The One True Philosophical Problem</b><br/><br/><i>"The Myth of Sisyphus"</i> purports to be about the <i>"one truly philosophical problem [of] suicide".</i><br/><br/>Perhaps, it's a little sensationalist to define the problem in these terms, at least in the 21st century. Even Camus himself immediately restated the problem as <i>"judging whether life is or is not worth living".</i><br/><br/>Maybe another way is to ask whether, if life is not worth living, does it follow that we should cease to live, e.g., by committing suicide? (It's interesting how</span>
  <span id="freeText2045981194290321012" style="display:none"><b>The One True Philosophical Problem</b><br><br><i>"The Myth of Sisyphus"</i> purports to be about the <i>"one truly philosophical problem [of] suicide".</i><br><br>Perhaps, it's a little sensationalist to define the problem in these terms, at least in the 21st century. Even Camus himself immediately restated the problem as <i>"judging whether life is or is not worth living".</i><br><br>Maybe another way is to ask whether, if life is not worth living, does it follow that we should cease to live, e.g., by committing suicide? (It's interesting how we <i>commit</i> four things: errors, crimes, sins, suicide.)<br><br>Camus tends to assume that, in the absence of God, there is no meaning of life, at least no superimposed, objective meaning of life.<br><br>Thus, for him, the resulting absurdity is the starting point, not the result of a deductive process.<br><br>If life is truly meaningless, the question is how to respond?<br><br>Do we revert to the meaning of life posited by religion and a supernatural being (an irrational response)? Do we commit suicide in order to escape the absence of meaning (the result of despair)? Or do we embrace the absurdity implicit in an absence of meaning without accepting it (revolt)?<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1440309839i/15959909._SY540_.jpg" width="400" height="500" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><i>Franz von Stuck's "Sisyphus" (1920)</i><br><br><br><b>The Confrontation</b><br><br>For Camus, we long for meaning. Yet, we don't readily find it. Partly because it isn't there. The absurd is born of <i>"the confrontation between the human need and the unreasonable silence of the world".</i> (29)<br><br>The absurd is a divorce: <i>"It lies in neither of the elements compared; it is born of their confrontation."</i><br><br>And what is the confrontation between? In effect, <i>"the Absurd is not in man...nor in the world, but in their presence together."</i> (30) Absurdity describes a relationship between the two.<br><br>Not just is the Absurd a confrontation, but it is also an <i>"unceasing struggle",</i> which struggle <i>"implies a total absence of hope, a continual rejection and a conscious dissatisfaction...A man devoid of hope and conscious of being so has ceased to belong to the future."</i> (31)<br><br>Arguably, a man with no hope has no reason to continue living into the future. Without hope, what awaits us is inevitable death (which awaits us anyway, with or without hope).<br><br><b>The Escape</b><br><br>Camus considers that all existentialist attempts to deal with the Absurd <i>"suggest escape...they deify what crushes them and find reason to hope in what impoverishes them."</i><br><br>He maintains that <i>"nothing logically prepares this reason. I can call it a leap."</i> Paradoxically, it shares something with a religious leap of faith: <i>"we turn towards God only to obtain the impossible. As for the possible, men suffice."</i> (33)<br><br>Nevertheless, the leap is an escape. By it, we seek to elude the Absurd.<br><br><b>Endurance</b><br><br>In contrast, Camus argues that <i>"living is keeping the absurd alive."</i> (47)<br><br>We must keep it alive so that we can confront and endure it. To do so, we must revolt against it:<br><br><i>"It is a constant confrontation between man and his own obscurity. It is an insistence upon an impossible transparency...metaphysical revolt extends awareness to the whole of experience...Revolt gives life its value. Spread out over the whole of a life, it restores its majesty to that life. To a man devoid of blinkers, there is no finer sight than that of the intelligence at grips with a reality that transcends it."</i>(47)<br><br>Camus' solution therefore is consciousness and revolt. (48)<br><br>Suicide is an illusory solution:<br><br><i>"It is essential to die unreconciled [to the Absurd] and not of one's own free will."</i> (49)<br><br><b>The Revolt</b><br><br>According to Camus, man must never surrender or give in. We must live <i>"without appeal"</i> to some greater natural or supernatural authority. Only then are we truly free and responsible. (52)<br><br>Camus sees the future, inevitably, as an invitation to death. However, he converts the revolt, the refusal to commit suicide, into a rule of life.<br><br>The Absurd therefore gives us three qualities: our revolt, our freedom, and our passion (for life over death). (55)<br><br>Camus distinguishes between <i>"renunciation"</i> and <i>"revolt".</i> <br><br><i>"Renunciation"</i> is an irrational denial of the absurd, e.g., like religion. Camus writes <i>"consciousness and revolt, these rejections are the contrary of renunciation."</i> Rejection doesn't deny the existence of the absurd, whereas renunciation does.<br><br><b>"The Point is to Live"</b><br><br>These arguments define a metaphysical process, a way of thinking. However, Camus concludes, <i>"The point is to live."</i> (56) We must live without appeal, but informed of our limits. (57)<br><br>It is <i>"essential to elude nothing. There is thus a metaphysical honour in enduring the world's absurdity. Conquest or play-acting, multiple loves, absurd revolt are tributes that man pays to his dignity in a campaign in which he is defeated in advance."</i> (77)<br><br>There is honour in battle, honour in confrontation, honour in revolt.<br><br><b>Metaphysical Art and Literature</b><br><br>Camus finds sustenance in art: <br><br><i>"The great novelists are philosophical novelists...what distinguishes modern sensibility from classical sensibility is that the latter thrives on moral problems and the former on metaphysical problems."</i> (85)<br><br>For me, the focus on the metaphysical points to a bridge between modernism and post-modernism. Both are separate from the realist focus on morality, on problems of good and evil. <br><br>Art is fundamental to our pursuit of freedom in the short time we have on earth. In art, we find <i>"not the divine fable that amuses and blinds, but the terrestrial face, gesture, and drama in which are summed up a difficult wisdom and an ephemeral passion."</i> (95)<br><br>Art captures the ephemeral flame that burns passionate and bright for the duration of our short sojourn. <br><br><b>The Myth of Sisyphus</b><br><br>It's here that Camus introduces the myth of Sisyphus. The burden of Sisyphus is his fate. Perhaps it is a futile and hopeless labour. However, <i>"all Sisyphus' silent joy is contained therein. His fate belongs to him. His rock is his thing."</i> (98)<br><br>In the same way, <i>"the absurd man, when he contemplates his torment, silences all the idols,"</i> the illusions that encourage him to elude Absurdity:<br><br><i>"There is no sun without shadow, and it is essential to know the night."</i><br><br>He who recognises this will be the master of his days:<br><br><i>"The struggle itself towards the heights is enough to fill a man's heart. One must imagine Sisyphus happy."</i> (99)<br><br>So too, we must imagine Sisyphus happy, if we are to be happy, because ultimately our burden is the same.<br><br><br><b>SOUNDTRAK:</b><br><br><b>Soul II Soul - "Get a Life"</b><br><br><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://m.youtube.com/watch?v=EezvP8PqV2k">https://m.youtube.com/watch?v=EezvP8P...</a><br><br><b>Soul II Soul - "Keep On Movin'"</b><br><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://m.youtube.com/watch?v=1iQl46-zIcM">https://m.youtube.com/watch?v=1iQl46-...</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="2045981194290321012" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1759552047?book_show_action=true">Sep 16, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Steven Godin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Steven Godin" href="/user/show/26706841-steven-godin">Steven Godin</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780141023991" href="/book/show/612957.The_Myth_of_Sisyphus">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26706841-steven-godin?shelf=existentialism">existentialism</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26706841-steven-godin?shelf=france">france</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26706841-steven-godin?shelf=non-fiction">non-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26706841-steven-godin?shelf=essays">essays</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26706841-steven-godin?shelf=philosophy">philosophy</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1759552047" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4296926274879312160">This was a fascinating insight into a thought provoking question, Albert Camus suggests that suicide amounts to a confession that life is not worth living. He links this confession to what he calls the "feeling of absurdity", that on the whole, we go through life with meaning and purpose, with a sense that we do things for good and profound reasons. Occasionally, however for some at least, we might come to see our daily lives dictated primarily by the forces of habit, thus bringing into question</span>
  <span id="freeText4296926274879312160" style="display:none">This was a fascinating insight into a thought provoking question, Albert Camus suggests that suicide amounts to a confession that life is not worth living. He links this confession to what he calls the "feeling of absurdity", that on the whole, we go through life with meaning and purpose, with a sense that we do things for good and profound reasons. Occasionally, however for some at least, we might come to see our daily lives dictated primarily by the forces of habit, thus bringing into question the following, if one feels that the embodiment of freedom is lost to a drone-like existence, all of our actions and reasons for them to a degree become pointless, with a feeling of absurdity linked to meaningless, meaningless to death by ones own hand. Camus in basic terms simply implies that we start to live before the habit of thinking on a deep level takes hold, thus avoiding the consequences of the meaningless nature of life, through what Camus calls an "act of eluding.", we choose not to think about the absurd because our nature is built on that of hopes and dreams for a meaningful life rather than face the consequences of staring into the void.<br />One the main attributes used throughout his fiction, that of "exile" is also included heavily as a comparative for this essay. No one else but Camus could have wrote this work, as soon as you enter his world, the world around you becomes less apparent. <br /> </span>
  <a data-text-id="4296926274879312160" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_3012753218" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3012753218" />
    <a title="Matthew Appleton" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/57003346-matthew-appleton"><img alt="Matthew Appleton" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1578773686p2/57003346.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3012753218?book_show_action=true">Jul 30, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Matthew Appleton" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Matthew Appleton" href="/user/show/57003346-matthew-appleton">Matthew Appleton</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/57003346-matthew-appleton?shelf=favourites">favourites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/57003346-matthew-appleton?shelf=read-2020">read-2020</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/57003346-matthew-appleton?shelf=french-literature">french-literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/57003346-matthew-appleton?shelf=nobel-writers">nobel-writers</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/57003346-matthew-appleton?shelf=essays">essays</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/57003346-matthew-appleton?shelf=non-fiction">non-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/57003346-matthew-appleton?shelf=foreign-translated">foreign-translated</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/57003346-matthew-appleton?shelf=20th-century">20th-century</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/57003346-matthew-appleton?shelf=philosophy">philosophy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3012753218" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3514143228025022824">120th book of 2020. <br/><br/>I’m a pretty huge Velvet Underground fan, and by extension, a big Lou Reed fan. Anyone else in the same position may have seen young Reed causing ‘intellectual havoc’ in his interviews. He speaks in paradoxes. In 1974 Lou sits in my favourite interview of mine, being difficult, partly, but also being astoundingly clever. <br/><br/>“Lou, you’re a man of few words, why is this?”<br/>“I don’t have anything to say.”<br/>“Do you like meeting people, talking to people?”<br/>“Some.”<br/>“Do you like talking to u</span>
  <span id="freeText3514143228025022824" style="display:none">120th book of 2020. <br /><br />I’m a pretty huge Velvet Underground fan, and by extension, a big Lou Reed fan. Anyone else in the same position may have seen young Reed causing ‘intellectual havoc’ in his interviews. He speaks in paradoxes. In 1974 Lou sits in my favourite interview of mine, being difficult, partly, but also being astoundingly clever. <br /><br />“Lou, you’re a man of few words, why is this?”<br />“I don’t have anything to say.”<br />“Do you like meeting people, talking to people?”<br />“Some.”<br />“Do you like talking to us?”<br />“I don’t know you.”<br />“Do you like press interviews in general?”<br />“No.”<br />“Do you shun publicity?”<br />“No.”<br /><br />And when frankly asked if he was a “transvestite or homosexual”, he answered, “Sometimes.” <br /><br />I mention this because Camus’ philosophy here is partially paradoxical. In another Lou Reed interview he allows us to look briefly behind the stage of his mind: An interviewer notes that he is talking in paradoxes, and Lou Reed answers:<br /><br />“When faced with a paradox, I become paradoxical.” <br /><br />But, to be paradoxical is not always a bad thing. The famous opening line of the title essay is simply, <i>There is but one truly serious philosophical problem and that is suicide.</i> Camus also gives us the opportunity to consider that Sisyphus is a <i>happy man</i>. Standing at the bottom of the hill with his rock, he cannot be happy; he is about to embark on the labour that he is bound to, forever. At the top of the hill, however, he is surely not feeling the same way. Camus writes, <i>At the very end of his long effort measured by skyless space and time without depth, the purpose is achieved. Then Sisyphus watches the stone rush down in a few moments towards that lower world whence he will have to push it up again towards the summit. He goes back down to the plain.</i> I suppose, in a sense, in thinking about the unimaginable torture of pushing this rock up a hill for the rest of time, Camus is reminding us that it is not all torture. Simply, there are moments, many moments, that he is ‘achieving’, that he has completed task… In those seconds before it rolls back down again. <i>At each of those moments when he leaves the heights and gradually sinks towards the lairs of the gods, he is superior to his fate. He is stronger than his rock.</i> Sisyphus, for Camus, represents the human condition. <br /><br />The dictionary definition of ‘paradox’ is this: <br /><br />a seemingly absurd or contradictory statement or proposition which when investigated may prove to be well founded or true.<br /><br />Forgetting the definition for a moment, let us look at that all important third word. Absurd. These essays are perfect companions to <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/49552.The_Stranger" title="The Stranger by Albert Camus" rel="nofollow noopener">The Stranger</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/11990.The_Rebel" title="The Rebel by Albert Camus" rel="nofollow noopener">The Rebel</a>, these books are exploring absurdism. The title essay is split into chapters, per say, of: “An Absurd Reasoning”, “The Absurd Man”, “Absurd Creation”, “The Myth of Sisyphus” and finally, the “Appendix” (which is titled “Hope and the Absurd in the Work of Franz Kafka”). Camus is not talking about only suicide here. He is talking about literature, art, the self, suicide, yes, but suicide can only be discussed in relation to life, for, of course, it is the taking of life. He discusses Dostoevsky, Kafka, he calls Melville an absurd writer… And he discusses the Absurd Man, and the Absurd Hero. Considering Camus’ characteristics of the Absurd Man, can we truly call him innocent? Camus suggests that we can.<br /><br />The Absurd Hero lives without purpose, and through his own scope of philosophy, that there is no truth. There is contradiction in living without a purpose. Though I do not agree with all of Camus’ philosophies, the Absurd is actually more significant to me than I first believed. Consider his take on religion: Camus once said, “I do not believe in God and I am not an atheist.” I have said the exact same thing, many times in my life. I believe and I don’t believe. I am and I am not. In the end, I realise, I possibly sounded quite like Lou Reed. When faced with a paradox (life), I become paradoxical. My mother has always said to me, on religion, “God is God is God is God” for however long she feels like repeating it. Sisyphus is the perfect Absurd Hero; his only accomplishments for the rest of eternity are rooted in his torture, and his only existence. In the end, Camus argues that he knows “himself to be the master of his days.” And for that reason, Camus believes he can be happy.<br /><br />The question for us, as the reader, is what does this mean for ourselves? What does that mean for our world? <i>Nothing else for the moment but indifference to the future and desire to use up everything that is given.</i> Ironically, the answer, answering with the Absurd, makes the world more Absurd again. It is depressing and comforting at once – God may exist, but that makes suffering no clearer. The other famous example: We can fill life with friends and family and love, but what does any of that mean when they die, we die, that people get diseases, die in freak accidents? In the simplest of terms, Camus’ meaning of life is accepting that there is no meaning in life. Not so comforting. Or is it? My mother often admits she can never be ‘happy’ because she is a perfectionist; nothing can be perfect, therefore she can never be happy. Absurd, I tell her. And she says, Yes. And though she accepts that nothing can be perfect, she continues to <i>strive</i> for perfection. In that sense, my mother is like Sisyphus – the Absurd Hero. There is no ‘meaning’ to striving for perfection because it is impossible, but she continues living, because she has found a purpose in striving for it regardless of its plausibility. In fact, turning the viewfinder on myself, am I not almost the same? I wish to be a writer, I wake every day and write in hope of writing a ‘good’ novel, possibly the ‘best’ novel, but that is not possible. Trying is what makes life worth living; if we stand at the bottom of the hill every day and begin pushing the rock up we may believe that our life is pointless and fruitless; unhappy. Or, we will stand at the bottom and know that our <i>purpose</i> is to drive that rock upwards, and when we reach the top, we have, again, momentarily, succeeded. <br /><br />Lou Reed seems to be some sort of chameleon: “When faced with a paradox, I become paradoxical.” Sisyphus finds meaning in the meaningless. My mother finds small accomplishments in the unaccomplishable – perfection. In the end, for Camus, life is Absurd: people die, people live with broken hearts, people grieve, people cry, people lose themselves; this is all to be accepted and embraced. Life is the meaning we place in it, to whatever end; possibly it doesn’t matter. Sisyphus is happy when he considers his ‘goal’, his ‘purpose’, not the eternity that stretches out before him. Camus' distinction: <i>if I admit that my freedom has no meaning except in relation to its limited fate, then I must say that what counts is not the best living but the most living</i>. So, mother, you will never make anything perfect, because nothing is perfect, but I hope that you are happy in trying, and that your life is then purposeful – the only, the greatest thing, we can maybe hope for.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3514143228025022824" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2214891582?book_show_action=true">Apr 13, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="P.E." class="user" itemprop="url" name="P.E." href="/user/show/68429005-p-e">P.E.</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9782070322886" href="/book/show/15714.Le_mythe_de_Sisyphe">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/68429005-p-e?shelf=essay">essay</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/68429005-p-e?shelf=absurd">absurd</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2214891582" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16557983536146819956"><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1528985479i/25778439._SX540_.jpg' height='400' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/>- <b>
  <i>Le Penseur, de Bernard et Clotilde Barto - near La Médiathèque Jacques Demy, Nantes</i>
</b><br/><br/>Right after <b>Promise at Dawn</b> (<b>La Promesse de l'Aube</b>), I wrap up <b>The myth of Sisyphus</b> and come out eventually disheartened by the mighty silence ruling over the studio in Lorient. In spare words, this is a study on the absurd. The onset is : "is life worth living?" The subject is tailored to make you react to it and decide where you stand.<br/><br/>On the whole, I don't align with Camus. I am astounded by the sternness </span>
  <span id="freeText16557983536146819956" style="display:none"><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1528985479i/25778439._SX540_.jpg" height="400" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>- <b>
  <i>Le Penseur, de Bernard et Clotilde Barto - near La Médiathèque Jacques Demy, Nantes</i>
</b><br><br>Right after <b>Promise at Dawn</b> (<b>La Promesse de l'Aube</b>), I wrap up <b>The myth of Sisyphus</b> and come out eventually disheartened by the mighty silence ruling over the studio in Lorient. In spare words, this is a study on the absurd. The onset is : "is life worth living?" The subject is tailored to make you react to it and decide where you stand.<br><br>On the whole, I don't align with Camus. I am astounded by the sternness of his observations. Indeed, they are accountable to the aim Camus sets but they entirely negate joy and <i>ivresse</i>, together with whatever personal purpuse and illusion they may bear to you.<br><br>Camus writes :<br><br>"<i>What is absurd is the meeting of the irrational with the craving and the call for clarity which resonates in the innermost depths of man.</i>"<br><br>("<i>Ce qui est absurde, c'est la confrontation de cet irrationnel et de ce désir éperdu de clarté dont l'appel résonne au plus profond de l'homme</i>") <br><b>Le mythe de Sisyphe, Folio essais (1942)</b>, p.36<br><br>"<i>If I were a tree among trees, a cat among animals, life would have purpose, or to put it in other words, this problem wouldn't have, because I would be part of the world. I would be the world against which I set myself with my whole conscience and by my requiring it to be kindred</i>".<br><br>("<i>Si j'étais arbre parmi les arbres, chat parmi les animaux, cette vie aurait un sens, ou plutôt ce problème n'en aurait point car je ferais partie de ce monde. Je <i>serais</i> ce monde auquel je m'oppose maintenant par toute ma conscience et par toute mon exigence de familiarité</i>")<br><b>Le mythe de Sisyphe, Folio essais (1942)</b>, p.76<br><br>Although, where did Camus's absurd man come from? Like <i>a tree among trees</i>, he comes from a state of implied oneness with the world, he once lived in oneness. This state is by no means gratuitous. Once, there was a link, a kinship with the world, now reigns silence. This is, I guess, what could lead to such dereliction.<br><br>Camus states : <br>"<i>Absurd is born of the meeting of the human desire with the insensate silence of the world.</i>"<br><br>("<i>L'absurde naît de la confrontation de l'appel humain avec le silence déraisonnable du monde</i>")<br><b>Le mythe de Sisyphe, Folio essais (1942)</b>, p.46<br><br>It sounds short-sighted though : the world may well be insensate, unreasonable, irrationnal, but this delirium is <b>by no means silent.<br><br>Let us agree the world has no meaning whatsoever by itself. But to hold to you can't construe a personal, makeshift, limited purpose out of the world as you meet people in this delirium, is untrue. And out of the meager purpose that could weld out of this insensate world, I know friendship is the most tangible and vital happening.<br><br>So like Chestov and Husserl, who did consent to considerable crookedness to ram in their demonstrations and force their conclusions, as to where I stand, I think Camus's scepticism have him discard some elementary truths to uphold the <i>passion of the absurd</i> to the end. </b><br><br>----------<br><br>Je boucle le mythe de Sisyphe après La promesse de l'aube et j'en ressors accablé par le silence souverain d'un studio à Lorient.<br><br>Je ne rejoins pas entièrement Camus dans des observations qui me surprennent par leur aridité d'ensemble, explicables par le but qu'il se donne mais qui nient la joie avec ce qu'elle comporte d'ivresse, de sens propre à chacun, d'illusion si on veut. <br><br>Camus écrit :<br>"Ce qui est absurde, c'est la confrontation de cet irrationnel et de ce désir éperdu de clarté dont l'appel résonne au plus profond de l'homme" <br><b>Le mythe de Sisyphe, Folio essais (1942)</b>, p.36<br><br>"Si j'étais arbre parmi les arbres, chat parmi les animaux, cette vie aurait un sens, ou plutôt ce problème n'en aurait point car je ferais partie de ce monde. Je <i>serais</i> ce monde auquel je m'oppose maintenant par toute ma conscience et par toute mon exigence de familiarité"<br><b>Le mythe de Sisyphe, Folio essais (1942)</b>, p.76<br><br>Et pourtant, comme l'arbre parmi les arbres, l'homme absurde de Camus était parti d'un état d'unité tacite avec le monde. Ce premier état n'a rien de négligeable. Là où il y avait d'abord un lien, le silence ensuite. Voilà ce qui paraît conduire à la perte de sens. <br><br>Camus soutient :<br>"L'absurde naît de la confrontation de l'appel humain avec le silence déraisonnable du monde"<br><b>Le mythe de Sisyphe, Folio essais (1942)</b>, p.46<br><br>Il n'y a qu'un malheur : si le monde est déraisonnable, ce délire fait de chimères, d'ivresses et de rencontres n'a <b>rien</b> de silencieux.<br><br>Le monde n'a aucun sens intelligible par lui-même, mettons. De là à soutenir qu'on ne peut pas construire un sens provisoire au monde au cours des rencontres, il y a loin, et du sens qui filtre de ce monde déraisonnable, l'amitié me paraît la manifestation la plus tangible et la plus vivante.<br><br><br>Comme Chestov et Husserl qui consentent à des entorses considérables pour aboutir de force à leurs conclusions, je crois aujourd'hui que le scepticisme de Camus le pousse à sacrifier quelques vérités élémentaires pour soutenir <i>la passion de l'absurde</i> jusqu'à son terme logique.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16557983536146819956" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
