<div id="review_105468489" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/105468489" />
    <a title="Rowland Pasaribu" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3564385-rowland-pasaribu"><img alt="Rowland Pasaribu" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1513834488p2/3564385.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/105468489?book_show_action=true">Jun 03, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rowland Pasaribu" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rowland Pasaribu" href="/user/show/3564385-rowland-pasaribu">Rowland Pasaribu</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer105468489" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1552973583649812522">Albert Camus (1913–1960) is not a philosopher so much as a novelist with a strong philosophical bent. He is most famous for his novels of ideas, such as The Stranger and The Plague, both of which are set in the arid landscape of his native Algeria.<br/><br/>Camus studied philosophy at the University of Algiers, which brought him into contact with two of the major branches of twentieth century philosophy: existentialism and phenomenology. Existentialism arises from an awareness that there is no pre-ordaine</span>
  <span id="freeText1552973583649812522" style="display:none">Albert Camus (1913–1960) is not a philosopher so much as a novelist with a strong philosophical bent. He is most famous for his novels of ideas, such as The Stranger and The Plague, both of which are set in the arid landscape of his native Algeria.<br /><br />Camus studied philosophy at the University of Algiers, which brought him into contact with two of the major branches of twentieth century philosophy: existentialism and phenomenology. Existentialism arises from an awareness that there is no pre-ordained meaning or order in the universe and that we must take responsibility for determining the meaning and order we are to give to our lives. Camus is particularly interested in religious existentialists, such as Kierkegaard (though such a label is not entirely fair to Kierkegard), who conclude that there is no meaning to be found in human experience, and that this necessitates a "leap of faith" that places an irrational and blind faith in God.<br /><br />Phenomenology, as advocated by Edmund Husserl, confines itself to observing and describing our own consciousness without drawing any conclusions regarding causes or connections. Like existentialism, phenomenology influenced Camus by its effort to construct a worldview that does not assume that there is some sort of rational structure to the universe that the human mind can apprehend.<br /><br />This idea—that the universe has a rational structure that the mind can apprehend—characterizes an older trend in European philosophy called "rationalism." Rationalism traces its roots to Rene Descartes and to the birth of modern philosophy. Most of twentieth century European philosophy has been a direct reaction to this older tradition, a reactionary attempt to explore the possibility that the universe has no rational structure for the mind to apprehend.<br /><br />Camus wrote The Myth of Sisyphus around the same time he wrote his first novel, The Stranger, at the beginning of World War II. Camus was working for the French Resistance in Paris at this time, far from his native Algeria. While it is never wise to reduce ideas to their autobiographical background, the circumstances in which this essay was written can help us understand its tone. The metaphor of exile that Camus uses to describe the human predicament and the sense that life is a meaningless and futile struggle both make a great deal of sense coming from a man, far from his home, who was struggling against a seemingly omnipotent and senselessly brutal regime.<br /><br />The central concern of The Myth of Sisyphus is what Camus calls "the absurd." Camus claims that there is a fundamental conflict between what we want from the universe (whether it be meaning, order, or reasons) and what we find in the universe (formless chaos). We will never find in life itself the meaning that we want to find. Either we will discover that meaning through a leap of faith, by placing our hopes in a God beyond this world, or we will conclude that life is meaningless. Camus opens the essay by asking if this latter conclusion that life is meaningless necessarily leads one to commit suicide. If life has no meaning, does that mean life is not worth living? If that were the case, we would have no option but to make a leap of faith or to commit suicide, says Camus. Camus is interested in pursuing a third possibility: that we can accept and live in a world devoid of meaning or purpose.<br /><br />As his starting point, Camus takes up the question of whether, on the one hand, we are free agents with souls and values, or if, on the other hand, we are just matter that moves about with mindless regularity. Reconciling these two equally undeniable perspectives is one of the great projects of religion and philosophy.<br /><br />One of the most obvious—and on reflection, one of the most puzzling—facts about human existence is that we have values. Having values is more than simply having desires: if I desire something, I quite simply want it and will try to get it. My values go beyond my desires in that by valuing something, I do not simply desire it, but I also somehow judge that that something ought to be desired. In saying that something ought to be desired, I am assuming that the world ought to be a certain way. Further, I only feel the world ought to be a certain way if it is not entirely that way already: if there was no such thing as murder it would not make sense for me to say that people should not commit murder. Thus, having values implies that we feel the world ought to be different from the way it is.<br /><br />Our capacity to see the world both as it is and as it ought to be allows us to look at ourselves in two very different lights. Most frequently, we see others and ourselves as willing, free agents, people who can deliberate and make choices, who can decide what's best and pursue certain ends. Because we have values it only makes sense that we should also see ourselves as capable of embodying those values. There would be no point in valuing certain qualities if we were incapable of acting to realize those qualities.<br /><br />While we generally take this outlook, there is also the outlook of the scientist, of trying to see the world quite simply as it is. Scientifically speaking, this is a world divested of values, made up simply of matter and energy, where mindless particles interact in predetermined ways. There is no reason to think that humans are any exception to the laws of science. Just as we observe the behavior of ants milling about, mindlessly following some sort of mechanical routine, we can imagine alien scientists might also observe us milling about, and conclude that our behavior is equally predictable and routine-oriented.<br /><br />The feeling of absurdity is effectively the feeling we get when we come to see ourselves in the second of these two alternative perspectives. This is a strictly objective worldview that looks at things quite simply as they are. Values are irrelevant to this worldview, and without values there seems to be no meaning and no purpose to anything we do. Without values, life has no meaning and there is nothing to motivate us to do one thing rather than another.<br /><br />Though we may never have tried to rationalize this feeling philosophically, the feeling of absurdity is one that we have all experienced at some point in our life. In moments of depression or uncertainty, we might shrug and ask, "what's the point of doing anything?" This question is essentially a recognition of absurdity, a recognition that, from at least one perspective, there is no point in doing anything.<br /><br />Camus often refers metaphorically to the feeling of absurdity as a place of exile. Once we have acknowledged the validity of the perspective of a world without values, of a life without meaning, there is no turning back. We cannot simply forget or ignore this perspective. The absurd is a shadow cast over everything we do. And even if we choose to live as if life has a meaning, as if there are reasons for doing things, the absurd will linger in the back of our minds as a nagging doubt that perhaps there is no point.<br /><br />It is generally supposed that this place of exile—the absurd—is uninhabitable. If there is no reason for doing anything, how can we ever do anything? The two main ways of escaping the feeling of absurdity are suicide and hope. Suicide concludes that if life is meaningless then it is not worth living. Hope denies that life is meaningless by means of blind faith.<br /><br />Camus is interested in finding a third alternative. Can we acknowledge that life is meaningless without committing suicide? Do we have to at least hope that life has a meaning in order to live? Can we have values if we acknowledge that values are meaningless? Essentially, Camus is asking if the second of the two worldviews sketched above is livable.<br /><br /><br />Camus is not a philosopher and he is not interested in engaging the aforementioned thinkers in an intellectual debate. As in the previous chapter, where he rejected rationalism, Camus is not trying to refute these thinkers. He does not give us arguments as to why their thinking is askew, but simply gives us reasons as to why he finds their thinking unsatisfying.<br /><br />Camus reduces the problem that interests him to two basic facts: first, that man expects and hopes to find some sort of meaning in the world, and second, that whatever meaning the world may have is concealed from man. It is important to note that Camus does not deny that God exists or that there is some inherent meaning or purpose behind everything. He simply claims that he has no way of knowing whether or not there is a God or meaning or purpose. His aim in The Myth of Sisyphus is to determine whether or not it is possible to live simply with what he knows. That is, can he live with those two basic facts, or does he need either to hope for something more (a God or meaning or purpose) or to commit suicide?<br /><br />The absurd is the relationship that links these two basic facts. It is absurd that I should expect the universe to have a meaning when the universe itself is so resolutely silent. Because the absurd is the relationship that links the only two basic facts we can know for certain, Camus asserts that the absurd is our fundamental relationship with the world. The absurd is a fundamental truth and Camus takes it as his duty to follow out its logic.<br /><br />The absurd is also essentially a conflict. We demand meaning but the universe gives us none. The dissatisfaction we feel with our lot in life is fundamental to the absurd, and any attempt to resolve this dissatisfaction is an attempt to escape from absurdity.<br /><br />Camus's complaint against the four thinkers discussed in this chapter is that, each in his own way attempts to escape from absurdity. To do this, each thinker must reject one of the two basic facts that Camus has taken as his starting point. Jaspers, Chestov, and Kierkegaard reject the need for reason and purpose in the world. They embrace the idea that the world is irrational, and find God in this idea. Husserl rejects the idea that we cannot find meaning in the world, claiming to find essences behind its mute phenomena.<br /><br />Camus is not a philosopher, and he is not accusing these thinkers of reasoning wrongly. He is simply accusing them of not finding content in what they can know. All four go beyond the basic, undeniable facts of experience to assert that there is something more, something transcendent, something that resolves the dissatisfaction caused by their confrontation with the absurd. They are not mistaken in doing so, but they are avoiding the question that seems to Camus to be fundamental: do weneed to assert that there is something more in order to live? Camus's problem is a hypothetical one: if there is nothing more than rational humans in an irrational universe, can we live with the absurdity of that situation?<br /><br />The route Camus takes here is committed to shunning philosophy. He purports to be interested only in whether a certain proposition is livable, not whether it is true. If he were to try to assert his own metaphysical position, if he were to try to claim that such-and-such is the case, he would then be burdened with the responsibility of proving the superiority of his metaphysical position over those of other philosophers.<br /><br />All this is relevant because Camus comes dangerously close to metaphysics when he asserts that the absurd is our fundamental relationship with the world and that our need for reasons and the silence of the universe are the two basic facts of human existence. Camus might defend himself by saying that these assertions do not come from any positive knowledge about the nature of the world, but are rather all that is left over when he denies himself any positive knowledge. The absurd is our fundamental relationship with the world because it does not rely on claims to know anything about the world beyond what is given to us. Is it… :D</span>
  <a data-text-id="1552973583649812522" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1605782519" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1605782519" />
    <a title="Sanjay Gautam" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/10179929-sanjay-gautam"><img alt="Sanjay Gautam" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1427923732p2/10179929.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1605782519?book_show_action=true">Jul 05, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sanjay Gautam" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sanjay Gautam" href="/user/show/10179929-sanjay-gautam">Sanjay Gautam</a>
      </span>

        rated it

<span id="freeTextContainer16049223764733812576">I read this book shortly after Albert Camus' death when he was at the height of his popularity. As I was in high school, it may have been the first philosophical work that that I ever read. By the time I arrived at university three years later, the academics were hooting at it. The pedants asserted that the work demonstrated only the extent to which Camus the novelist was out of his depth as a philosopher.<br/><br/>I do not think that many of the profs from my era foresaw that Camus' works would have a bi</span>
  <span id="freeText16049223764733812576" style="display:none">I read this book shortly after Albert Camus' death when he was at the height of his popularity. As I was in high school, it may have been the first philosophical work that that I ever read. By the time I arrived at university three years later, the academics were hooting at it. The pedants asserted that the work demonstrated only the extent to which Camus the novelist was out of his depth as a philosopher.<br /><br />I do not think that many of the profs from my era foresaw that Camus' works would have a bigger following in this century than those of his rival and foe Jean Paul Sartre. I think that this work has the great virtue of clarity. I recommend this work to any teenager who has read "L'étranger." It provides an excellent introduction for the young reader to philosophical writing.</span>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Khashayar Mohammadi" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Khashayar Mohammadi" href="/user/show/32374363-khashayar-mohammadi">Khashayar Mohammadi</a>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32374363-khashayar-mohammadi?shelf=not-for-me">not-for-me</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32374363-khashayar-mohammadi?shelf=french-lit">french-lit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32374363-khashayar-mohammadi?shelf=philosophy">philosophy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32374363-khashayar-mohammadi?shelf=essays">essays</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2201354247" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1572205671579141480">Oh Camus... Oh Camus... Why are your books always the hardest to write about?<br/><br/>I've had quite the relationship with Camus' body of work; from strong hatred to admiration to indifference to hatred to well... this...<br/><br/>"Opinionated" and "Name-dropper" are redundant words to describe a Philosopher or essayist, since they undermine the very foundation of a work of philosophy; but I'm afraid, in the case of Camus I just can't avoid them. I feel if "Name-dropping" was an Olympic sport, France would win the</span>
  <span id="freeText1572205671579141480" style="display:none">Oh Camus... Oh Camus... Why are your books always the hardest to write about?<br /><br />I've had quite the relationship with Camus' body of work; from strong hatred to admiration to indifference to hatred to well... this...<br /><br />"Opinionated" and "Name-dropper" are redundant words to describe a Philosopher or essayist, since they undermine the very foundation of a work of philosophy; but I'm afraid, in the case of Camus I just can't avoid them. I feel if "Name-dropping" was an Olympic sport, France would win the gold every time. I understand that Camus is not even French, but I found his rigid analysis of certain prominent philosophers (and Chestov for some bloody reason!) rather disheartening. <br /><br />I am not the most clever or introspective reader, so please correct me if I have misunderstood; but the word "Absurd" was used continuously, and its ambiguity only grew with every subsequent use. "Absurd" became an umbrella term to refer to an entire range of irrational decisions, sublime moments and paradoxical behaviour that did not converge into a coherent concept for the purpose of the arguments provided, and before he gave us any thorough explanations or justifications for his arguments, he drew on the works of other philosophers, inserting his concept of the "Absurd" into their philosophical vocabulary.<br /><br />The most exciting aspect of the book came in the form of an easter egg hunt for the phrase "A Current Philosopher", which, in a book that names half a dozen philosophers per page, signified one person and one person only: Jean-Paul Sartre.<br /><br />I don't consider myself to be an attentive reader, but I am genuinely tired of giving Camus second chances. I refuse to labour endlessly to understand a single page, and if a philosopher's work throws me off, Instead of endlessly mining for rhyme and reason (which readers of Camus LOVE to do non-stop), I will label it as "Mediocre"</span>
  <a data-text-id="1572205671579141480" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1526956029" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1526956029" />
    <a title="Junta" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/38230974-junta"><img alt="Junta" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1447099175p2/38230974.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1526956029?book_show_action=true">Jan 26, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Junta" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Junta" href="/user/show/38230974-junta">Junta</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780141023991" href="/book/show/612957.The_Myth_of_Sisyphus">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Those who have read some philosophical works before</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Junta by: </span>
      The Stranger, The Plague, feeling existential
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/38230974-junta?shelf=non-fiction">non-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/38230974-junta?shelf=french">french</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/38230974-junta?shelf=translated">translated</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/38230974-junta?shelf=philosophy">philosophy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1526956029" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1974971296733238784">Hallelujah, I've finished. I think this was the slowest pace at which I read a book since joining Goodreads. For now (and possibly for eternity), three points:<br/><br/>1. if I were Sisyphus, a good punishment the gods could deal out to me would be to ceaselessly make me re-read this for eternity;<br/>2. as much as I struggled with this book, I don't regret picking it up - as Calvino says, <i>Every new book I read comes to be a part of that overall and unitary book that is the sum of my readings...</i> things won't b</span>
  <span id="freeText1974971296733238784" style="display:none">Hallelujah, I've finished. I think this was the slowest pace at which I read a book since joining Goodreads. For now (and possibly for eternity), three points:<br /><br />1. if I were Sisyphus, a good punishment the gods could deal out to me would be to ceaselessly make me re-read this for eternity;<br />2. as much as I struggled with this book, I don't regret picking it up - as Calvino says, <i>Every new book I read comes to be a part of that overall and unitary book that is the sum of my readings...</i> things won't be so easy for you next time, philosophy; and<br />3. I want to read <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/333538.The_Castle" title="The Castle by Franz Kafka" rel="nofollow noopener">The Castle</a> in the near future. The last 15 pages of this book (the Appendix) were on <i>Hope and The Absurd in the Work of Franz Kafka</i>.<br /><br />Mid-reading blabbering (February 24, 2016): <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[I was going to save writing a (non-?) review on this book until I've finished it, but it's been taking me longer than I expected, and perhaps more importantly, I seem to be an insomniac tonight, so I have decided to blabber into the cool, dark night that is the world around me at present. This is the first philosophical essay I've picked up - I've had a rather confused relationship with philosophy in general, as if I'm trying to add things up in my head without having ever learnt the notion of the plus-sign, or I just didn't comprehend what people see as attractive in a film. I feel I'm missing something on a fundamental level that most people have no trouble with.<br /><br />During my undergraduate, I took a number of philosophy courses - it seemed like something I should try, as someone who lives inside their head most of the time. In my first philosophy course I took in my final year, mostly comprised of first-year students, we would look at the history of philosophy over the semester - introductory stuff, skimming over different philosophers, thoughts and movements in history each week.<br /><br />Now, so I don't paint an overly bleak picture of my life, I am going to note in advance that in the courses I found interesting through university, I was an active contributor to discussions. I was quite embarrassed about this at the time, but now I can wryly list it as a proud achievement - over the whole semester with this course, I did not open my mouth once during the tutorials (weekly, discussion-based classes complementing the lectures, with 15-20 students) to contribute to discussions. Materially, I suffered a little as 10% of our course grade was made up of this facet.<br /><br />My problem with discussions on philosophers and philosophical schools was, and has been this: how can we have personal opinions on big, unanswerable questions (presupposing an attitude of rational observation), especially ones which we are not very well acquainted with, worth sharing? Of course, the overarching factor here would be that I have always detested sharing my thoughts to people I don't know well if I don't find it necessary (verbally - here I am writing to the world wide web). I naturally lean towards keeping my thoughts to myself.<br /><br />What I seem to struggle with in philosophy, and perhaps non-fiction overall, is that it posits interpretations, as opposed to fiction in which interpretation is completely open (or perhaps 'unnecessary'), and it is impressions which the pages bring to our souls instead. To have impressions or interpretations concerning impressions is fine, but to have them about interpretations - I think this may be what I struggle with. Reading about philosophy through my courses, I could never get past a detached interest, of 'well, sure, it's interesting, but...' But what? I'm still thinking about this question (read: I have some ideas but I'll save that for another time). This book has been no exception - I don't mind it, and I will finish it - but I struggle to find what I really think about it. I'll be groping around in the dark.<br /><br />Wise Goodreads friends, these lines have been all over the place, and I don't know for what purpose I've written this, other than to simply pen my thoughts - apologies if it's all been nonsensical. If you have any words that might help me please do not hesitate to write them. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />March 18, 2016</span>

But one day the “why” arises and everything begins in that weariness tinged with amazement.”</b><br/><br/>I love <b>Camus</b>! I just LOVE him!<br/>He has that weird ability to draw my full attention, and to make me lose the tra</span>
  <span id="freeText8324877782939676544" style="display:none">‎‫‏‬<img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1495747007i/22852986._SX540_.jpg" width="420" height="142" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg">‬‬‬‬‬‬<br><br><b>“It happens that the stage sets collapse. Rising, streetcar, four hours in the office or the factory, meal, streetcar, four hours of work, meal, sleep, and Monday Tuesday Wednesday Thursday Friday and Saturday according to the same rhythm – this path is easily followed most of the time. But one day the “why” arises and everything begins in that weariness tinged with amazement.”</b><br><br>I love <b>Camus</b>! I just LOVE him!<br>He has that weird ability to draw my full attention, and to make me lose the track of time whenever I read the books he wrote.<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1516969670ra/24935962.gif" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1516969670ra/24935961.gif" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1435677636ra/15369957.gif" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Years ago, I first met Camus when I read his magnificent novel <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/49552.The_Stranger" title="The Stranger by Albert Camus" rel="nofollow noopener">The Stranger</a>. Since then, I knew that I won’t stop reading for him until I finish all of his books – more than once for sure! Yes, the writer who forces me to read his books many times without feeling bored, is not just a writer – but <b>a true immortal inspiration</b>.<br><br><i>
  <u>Side Note:</u>
</i> It is unfortunate, though, to have only <b>MALE</b> favorite writers! I struggle to find a <b>FEMALE</b> favorite writer! This is a real trouble! And I’m afraid if it continued it will turn my gay hormones on over time! <br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1495630625ra/22839063.gif" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1516969670ra/24935963.gif" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Okay. <b>What is this book about</b>? <br>It talks about many things, most importantly Absurdity, Suicide, and the meaning of life. These are BIG topics, and the fact that Camus is the one who tackled them makes them much <b>BIG-GER</b>! You need to really <b>FOCUS</b> while reading these philosophical essays, and you need to be familiar with some existential and absurd writers, such as: Dostoyevsky and Kafka. Camus talks in length about them and discusses their novels and ideas in a very deep way. So, if you’re not familiar with those AMAZING writers, then I suggest you read some of their works before reading this book (I suggest <i>Dostoyevsky’s The Brothers Karamazov</i>, and <i>Kafka’s Metamorphosis</i> – at least!).<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1516969670i/24935960._SX540_.jpg" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1498666551i/23149990._SX540_.jpg" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>I’m truly happy right now because I had the chance to read this book (thanks to my dear friend Samra) and I’m really sad! Because this book literally <b>BLEW</b> my mind and <b>BROKE</b> my heart!<br><br>I recommend it. IT IS A MUST READ!</span>
  <a data-text-id="8324877782939676544" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_645975448" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/645975448" />
    <a title="S." class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7627658-s"><img alt="S." src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1580837923p2/7627658.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/645975448?book_show_action=true">Jan 21, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="S." class="user" itemprop="url" name="S." href="/user/show/7627658-s">S.</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7627658-s?shelf=2020">2020</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer645975448" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17200082590772004102">There's both a feeling of liberation and imprisonment in Camus' train of thought, a paradox with which I very much agree with.<br/>Liberation for the reassurance that you are aware of the disconcerting truth that death is the end, so you act accordingly (the mantra being, <i>to live as much/long as possible</i>); and imprisonment because no matter what you do, no matter how much you try to <i>seize the day</i>, you are condemned to, in a matter of time, dive into oblivion and nothingness.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2455226454" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2455226454" />
    <a title="Murtaza " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/13716226-murtaza"><img alt="Murtaza " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1531516448p2/13716226.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2455226454?book_show_action=true">Jul 13, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Murtaza " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Murtaza " href="/user/show/13716226-murtaza">Murtaza </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2455226454" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11760954363183604359">Camus thesis in this book is exemplified in its famous statement that, "there is but one truly serious philosophical problem and that is suicide." Like much modern existentialist philosophy, the guiding principle amounts to an assertion that the world is a desert, and then proceeds to question whether or not you can (or should) survive it. Is it even worth surviving, or trying to survive for the brief moment that we exist anyways? Why not, as many do today despite unprecedented material wealth, </span>
  <span id="freeText11760954363183604359" style="display:none">Camus thesis in this book is exemplified in its famous statement that, "there is but one truly serious philosophical problem and that is suicide." Like much modern existentialist philosophy, the guiding principle amounts to an assertion that the world is a desert, and then proceeds to question whether or not you can (or should) survive it. Is it even worth surviving, or trying to survive for the brief moment that we exist anyways? Why not, as many do today despite unprecedented material wealth, just end life right now if existence itself is a meaningless void, barren of the metaphysical gardens and springs that certain charlatans have baselessly promised?<br /><br />This book did not resonate with me personally, but only because I don't share its basic premises about reality. Having said that, I think that its impossible for a fair reader not to sympathize with Camus. One even has to applaud him for honestly trying to engage with the undeniable absurdity of a life lived in the face of a silently indifferent universe. In his book, "Islam Between East and West," Alija Ali Izetbegovic, commented sympathetically on the fundamentally religious nature of both nihilist and absurdist philosophy, of which The Myth of Sisyphus is a signal example:<br /><br /><i>Nihilism and the philosophy of the absurd are the fruits of the most civilized and richest parts of the world. That philosophy speaks of “the perspectiveless world,” of “the psychically split and <br />disintegrated individual, of the world of deaf mute silence.” It is by no means a poisonous philosophy, as it is called by some people. In fact, it is very deep and instructive. It is an expression of man’s resistance, of his nonconsent to the world that is growing in a manner opposite to the very image of his own. It is man’s revolt against the one-dimensional world of civilization. For the same reasons, some people found modem nihilism a kind of religion and, as we will see, such a concept is not groundless. <br /><br />They both represent a negation of materialism and embrace this world with the same idea. The primeval anxiousness, the views beyond the grave, the desparate searching for a way out of the world in which man is a stranger — these ideas are common to both of them. The difference lies in the fact that nihilism does not find the way out, while religion claims to have found it.</i><br /><br />If human beings, after all, are merely products of the earth, as "natural" as trees and ants, what need for any cripplingly anxious philosophy to grapple with that base fact? Camus seems to argue that we should embrace existence the same way a tree embraces it, to grow for the sake of growing. We should exist passionately and in a spirit of revolt against the absurdity of existence itself. The mere act of exertion, like Sisyphus pushing his giant boulder, is a grounds for ecstasy and contentment when we sacrifice hope for anything better - hope being the root of all despair.<br /><br />This is a tough desert to live in and I'm not sure that everyone can. Nonetheless I can see how it might provide strength and spiritual sustenance for some people. It may also incentivize an activist view of life that is conducive to material improvement. All in all this is a thoughtful and humane book, though deceptively dense for its short length.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11760954363183604359" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9789723827590" href="/book/show/8689238-o-mito-de-s-sifo">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/117399210-lu-s?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/117399210-lu-s?shelf=philosophy">philosophy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/117399210-lu-s?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3408788675" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9993756482415833728">"There is no more beautiful spectacle than the intelligence grappling with a reality which exceeds it." This quote from his book, The Myth of Sysiph, applies wonderfully to its author.<br/><br/>Reality is beyond us all, and the meaning of life is foreign to us. However, we do not all have the same relationship with it, the same way of doing or including ourselves.<br/><br/>Those who believe in God and have chosen religion to honour Him have chosen the easy way. Everything is explained by Him and in Him. Death is on</span>
  <span id="freeText9993756482415833728" style="display:none">"There is no more beautiful spectacle than the intelligence grappling with a reality which exceeds it." This quote from his book, The Myth of Sysiph, applies wonderfully to its author.<br /><br />Reality is beyond us all, and the meaning of life is foreign to us. However, we do not all have the same relationship with it, the same way of doing or including ourselves.<br /><br />Those who believe in God and have chosen religion to honour Him have chosen the easy way. Everything is explained by Him and in Him. Death is only an opening to eternity in His kingdom.<br /><br />For those who do not believe, the problem remains among the simple-minded. These do not express torments or questions. And in the end, happy the simple-minded, the kingdom of heaven is theirs. The famous parable connects them to the previous ones.<br /><br />Albert Camus, neither simple-minded, excuse a little, nor believer, but contemptuous of the grand philosophical theories which he knows well, especially in their contradiction, wants a human response to his state of mortal in need of being able to give meaning to the life. His answer to him is the absurd man. It is Sysiph condemned to push his rock towards the top of the mountain and to start over eternally each time he has come down again in the valley.<br /><br />"Great novelists are philosophical novelists." Albert Camus proves it to us with the myth of Sysiph that we will always read too quickly and too lightly as these pages are heavy with reflection.<br /><br />IF I meant an enormity, I would say that reading this book is essential for anyone who is passionate about the man and his work and wants to deepen his knowledge. You still have to be ready to walk a difficult path. Camus, novelist-philosopher or philosopher-novelist, the myth of Sysiph obliges us to the second formula. In any case, a concerned man, tortured by the meaning of life, gifted with courage and the talent to express it.<br /><br />So the death of Camus against a tree in 1960: accident, assassination or logical continuation of reasoning and conclusion of the absurd man. This reading widens the range of possibilities.</span>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_892829307" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/892829307" />
    <a title="Jonathan Terrington" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6313408-jonathan-terrington"><img alt="Jonathan Terrington" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1478230200p2/6313408.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/892829307?book_show_action=true">Mar 26, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jonathan Terrington" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jonathan Terrington" href="/user/show/6313408-jonathan-terrington">Jonathan Terrington</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 141182008" href="/book/show/12841454-the-myth-of-sisyphus">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6313408-jonathan-terrington?shelf=classic-literature">classic-literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6313408-jonathan-terrington?shelf=classics-challenge-3">classics-challenge-3</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6313408-jonathan-terrington?shelf=non-fiction">non-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6313408-jonathan-terrington?shelf=philosophy">philosophy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer892829307" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11655443987374263755"><br/>Mythology is a passion of mine and has been ever since I was a younger child - an age when I had much greater clarity of mind than I do now and was hampered less by outward influences. Therefore, to see Albert Camus write a sequence of differing essays which explore existentialism (whether he was truly an 'existentialist' is a matter of debate and conjecture but he was interested in existentialist concepts) in a manner that connects back to mythology was fascinating.<br/><br/>For those who are unaware, th</span>
  <span id="freeText11655443987374263755" style="display:none"><br />Mythology is a passion of mine and has been ever since I was a younger child - an age when I had much greater clarity of mind than I do now and was hampered less by outward influences. Therefore, to see Albert Camus write a sequence of differing essays which explore existentialism (whether he was truly an 'existentialist' is a matter of debate and conjecture but he was interested in existentialist concepts) in a manner that connects back to mythology was fascinating.<br /><br />For those who are unaware, the myth of Sisyphus does (as with any Greek or Roman myths) differ from source to source. Some say he escaped from Hades (or Pluto) on the pretext of gaining revenge upon his wife, but fell in love with being alive again. Naturally this was an affront to Hades (having a dead man alive in the world) and so Sisyphus was dragged back down into the Greek or Roman versions of hell. As I said there are other versions (some of which say that Sisyphus was punished for hubris and believing he was greater than Zeus) - but the key facet of the myth that Camus addresses is the punishment of Sisyphus. <br /><br />Sisyphus was set the eternal punishment of rolling an incredibly heavy stone, some kind of boulder, up a hill for the hellish equivalent of one day. Every day he rolled the boulder up the hill and every day the boulder returned to the bottom before he reached the top. It is an eternal punishment cycle that defeated Sisyphus in the regularity of its routine.<br /><br />What Camus writes about, therefore, in relation to the myth of Sisyphus, is the existential idea about the reality of the world. In his other books, such as <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/49552.The_Stranger" title="The Stranger by Albert Camus" rel="nofollow noopener">The Stranger</a> or <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/11991.The_Fall" title="The Fall by Albert Camus" rel="nofollow noopener">The Fall</a>, Camus explores the idea that life is abstract, further that it is absurd. It is this conceit that he details in this short collection of essays - the notion that life is absurd and full of mechanical repetition.<br /><br />We all, to Camus, are like Sisyphus, and the great humorous tragedy of life is to believe that, absurdly, we can set out into the face of overwhelming defeat and triumph. Personally, I must admit that existentialism itself, with the particularly negative bent it always seems to through up, is in itself meaningless to me, yet the ideas Camus is discussing here are fascinating and still applicable. Though I believe life has purpose, there are times where we all are Sisyphus. The point at which we reach the top of the hill and look back on where we came from, only to see the boulder crash back down and realise that we are stuck in the repetitive cycle of continuing to continue on.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="11655443987374263755" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1581888196" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1581888196" />
    <a title="Soycd" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/16638128-soycd"><img alt="Soycd" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1527549268p2/16638128.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1581888196?book_show_action=true">Mar 16, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Soycd" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Soycd" href="/user/show/16638128-soycd">Soycd</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1581888196" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1576940707184530585"><i><br/>There is but one truly serious philosophical problem, and that is suicide. Judging whether life is or is not worth living amounts to answering the fundamental question of philosophy.</i><br/><br/>It is in <i>The Myth of Sisyphus</i> where French author Albert Camus introduces his philosophy of the absurd: the idea that humankind as a whole is stuck in a perpetual loop made of futile efforts to try to find meaning in an absurd world. He illustrates his point with a renowned character from Greek mythology: Sisyphus wa</span>
  <span id="freeText1576940707184530585" style="display:none"><i><br />There is but one truly serious philosophical problem, and that is suicide. Judging whether life is or is not worth living amounts to answering the fundamental question of philosophy.</i><br /><br />It is in <i>The Myth of Sisyphus</i> where French author Albert Camus introduces his philosophy of the absurd: the idea that humankind as a whole is stuck in a perpetual loop made of futile efforts to try to find meaning in an absurd world. He illustrates his point with a renowned character from Greek mythology: Sisyphus was condemned by the Gods to roll a rock to the top of a mountain, only to have it fall back to where it first begun and repeat the process an infinite number of times. <br /> <i><br />...It is during that return, that pause, that Sisyphus interests me. A face that toils so close to stones is already stone itself! I see that man going back down with a heavy yet measured step toward the torment of which he will never know the end. That hour like a breathing-space which returns as surely as his suffering, that is the hour of consciousness. At each of those moments when he leaves the heights and gradually sinks toward the lairs of the gods, he is superior to his fate. He is stronger than his rock.<br /><br />If this myth is tragic, that is because its hero is conscious. Where would his torture be, indeed, if at every step the hope of succeeding upheld him? The workman of today works every day in his life at the same tasks, and this fate is no less absurd. But it is tragic only at the rare moments when it becomes conscious. Sisyphus, proletarian of the gods, powerless and rebellious, knows the whole extent of his wretched condition: it is what he thinks of during his descent, the lucidity, that was to constitute his torture at the same time crowns his victory. There is no fate that cannot be surmounted by scorn.</i><br /><br />Camus argues that the only way out of the loop is to get rid of apathy and revolt against the established order. Suicide, to him, does not constitute a rebellious act but one of passivity and defeat. <br /><i><br />Living is keeping the absurd alive.<br /><br />That revolt is the certainly of a crushing fate, without the resignation that ought to accompany it.<br /></i><br />He analyzes the work of philosophers like Franz Kafka and Kierkegaard but it's his admiration for Dostoevsky what really caught my attention:<br /><i><br />All of Dostoevsky’s heroes question themselves as to the meaning of life. In this they are modern: they do not fear ridicule. What distinguishes modern sensibility from classical sensibility is that the latter thrives on moral problems and the former on metaphysical problems. In Dostoevsky’s novels the question is propounded with such intensity that it can only invite extreme solutions. Existence is illusory or it is eternal. If Dostoevsky were satisfied with this inquiry, he would be a philosopher. But he illustrates the consequences that such intellectual pastimes may have in a man’s life, and in this regard he is an artist. </i><br /><br />Especially this part that helps me understand why he was so adamant not to be called an <i>existentialist</i> and instead decided to embrace the <i>absurdist</i> label. <br /><i><br />Dostoevsky’s reply is humiliation, “shame” according to Stavrogin. An absurd work, on the contrary, does not provide a reply; that is the whole difference. Let us note this carefully in conclusion: what contradicts the absurd in that work is not its Christian character, but rather its announcing a future life. It is possible to be Christian and absurd. There are examples of Christians who do not believe in a future life. </i><br /><br /> Reading Camus is always a challenge. Here we find him again pushing his ideas to the limit to take the readers out of their comfort zone and make them reconsider the way they perceive the world. What is most admirable for me is his sense of morality and perseverance. Albert was a very intelligent man and very commited to his political and philosophical ideas. Every one of his books provides us with a unique oportunity to get inside the mind of one of the most interesting and singular men in literature and we should seize that. <br /><i><br />Now I can broach the notion of suicide. It has already been felt what solution might be given. At this point the problem is reversed. It was previously a question of finding out whether or not life had to have a meaning to be lived. It now becomes clear, on the contrary, that it will be lived all the better if it has no meaning. Living an experience, a particular fate, is accepting it fully. Now, no one will live this fate, knowing it to be absurd, unless he does everything to keep before him that absurd brought to light by consciousness.</i><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="1576940707184530585" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_3405345680" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3405345680" />
    <a title="Roberta" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/27285494-roberta"><img alt="Roberta" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1592373713p2/27285494.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3405345680?book_show_action=true">Jun 22, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Roberta" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Roberta" href="/user/show/27285494-roberta">Roberta</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27285494-roberta?shelf=flew-through-it">flew-through-it</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27285494-roberta?shelf=art">art</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27285494-roberta?shelf=rereadable">rereadable</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27285494-roberta?shelf=existentialism">existentialism</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27285494-roberta?shelf=non-fiction">non-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27285494-roberta?shelf=philosophy">philosophy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27285494-roberta?shelf=absurd">absurd</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3405345680" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2712491111623518602">My internet decided to fuck off today, so I‘ve read The Myth of Sisyphus (gave it 4 stars cause the first part kind of flew over my head with the references to other philosophers but I’m looking forward to rereading it when I’m smarter, so I can give this 5 stars.).<br/><br/>See, I‘m starting to see a pattern, I seek refuge in Albert’s (yeah, we’re on first name basis) writing whenever something’s bothering me. When I picked up to reread The Plague, it was the time of peak COVID anxiety. What’s bothering </span>
  <span id="freeText2712491111623518602" style="display:none">My internet decided to fuck off today, so I‘ve read The Myth of Sisyphus (gave it 4 stars cause the first part kind of flew over my head with the references to other philosophers but I’m looking forward to rereading it when I’m smarter, so I can give this 5 stars.).<br /><br />See, I‘m starting to see a pattern, I seek refuge in Albert’s (yeah, we’re on first name basis) writing whenever something’s bothering me. When I picked up to reread The Plague, it was the time of peak COVID anxiety. What’s bothering me now? People (you have no idea how annoying is to dislike people but then be an extrovert). <br /><br />I mean people bother me on a daily basis, it’s nothing new but there’s this weird melancholy that comes and goes in waves and I absolutely loathe it because whenever it comes around I seem to be aware that most people don’t really care about understanding other people, which then creates this “lonely in a crowd” sort of feeling. Why do I so boldly make the statement that people don’t care about understanding others? Well, the correct way of saying it is that they don’t care about understanding others fully. They put you in a box and slap a vague trait on it. “This is my artsy friend”, “this is my friend that’s into sports”, “this is my w e i r d friend, watch out, she might say something strange!”<br /><br />I have a lot of problems with that sort of thinking and I’ve never understood those people who, when prompted to describe themselves, say a single word or a profession. Personally, I’ve always struggled to describe myself in a short way and not ramble. Why? Because I’m a human being, not a fucking lamp or something. “Yeah, that lamp’s white” and “Yeah, I study law” sound kind of the same to me tbh. And when other people, especially those who could even be considered friends think about you in such a way, it’s insulting. I remember one time when I was described just by the fact that I occasionally listen to a particular band. Dude, a vinyl player has more personality than me then.<br /> <br />As a teen I’ve always thought that stereotypes were awful and so I wanted to do sports, to be an artist, to do nerdy shit, to be this, to do that and on and on it went. Why? Just to fuck with people, just so they couldn’t describe me in a single word (it’s not that hard when you’re interested in most things). I’m either all of those things or none of them, you don’t get to pick apart my character as if it was made out of Legos. It’s me who gets to choose who I am.<br /><br />I really want to drive my point of not understanding how people think of themselves incredibly simply home. When we consume stories, be it in films, TV shows or books, as a critique we say that characters were too shallow, but damn, the shallowest fictional character probably has more depth than people who say that they “love coffee, travelling, and going out with friends”. I’m starting to think I’m too fucking dumb to understand how can people describe themselves like that, it’s just incomprehensible. A puddle after a drizzle has more depth, HONEY, YOU’RE GOING TO DRY OUT WHEN THE SUN COMES OUT.<br /><br />But here comes the shitty part. I don’t think that people care, they choose what’s convenient and roll with it. Even after years of knowing each other you can’t quite shake the descriptions they’ve ascribed to you. So whenever the wave of melancholy comes around I ask myself if it’s even worth trying to prove otherwise. After all, maybe I can be sufficiently described in a single word? Maybe all those guys, who are “just fun-loving guys” have gotten it right? Maybe when my mom says that I’m nearly 24 and I should act like an adult, maybe she’s right? But what’s it like to be an adult? I mean, I legally already am an adult, so what do you mean? Is it responsibility? I am responsible. When I was a kid I thought adults were serious and never had fun. Is that it then? Is being an adult having a 9-5 job? I’d rather just be me tbh.<br /><br />So I wanted to hear somebody say “No! You show them, even though it’s pointless, you show them” and that’s what I got. I don’t want to be a caricature of a human being, I refuse to be that. Even if it means that I’ll be misunderstood for the rest of my life (yes yes, very dramatic, I’m aware). Aaand pity party's over, everyone can get back to your regularly scheduled programs.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2712491111623518602" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_548781044" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/548781044" />
    <a title="Marc" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2876105-marc"><img alt="Marc" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1569160429p2/2876105.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/548781044?book_show_action=true">Feb 28, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Marc" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Marc" href="/user/show/2876105-marc">Marc</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9782070322886" href="/book/show/15714.Le_mythe_de_Sisyphe">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2876105-marc?shelf=philosophy">philosophy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2876105-marc?shelf=existentialism">existentialism</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer548781044" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6731240461915250493">Camus starts with the proposition that there is only one truly serious philosophical problem: suicide, namely the question of whether life is worth living or not. Personally, Camus chooses to live with absurdity. In various essays this theme is worked out in a rather cerebral and not always readable style. Other themes are: freedom, revolt, passion. <br/>The essay 'Le mythe de Sisyphe' covers but 6 pages. It begins with a summary of the faults of Sisyphus, which were nothing but an extreme craving fo</span>
  <span id="freeText6731240461915250493" style="display:none">Camus starts with the proposition that there is only one truly serious philosophical problem: suicide, namely the question of whether life is worth living or not. Personally, Camus chooses to live with absurdity. In various essays this theme is worked out in a rather cerebral and not always readable style. Other themes are: freedom, revolt, passion. <br />The essay 'Le mythe de Sisyphe' covers but 6 pages. It begins with a summary of the faults of Sisyphus, which were nothing but an extreme craving for life. Most interesting is the mental state of Sisyphus while he descends the slope. The tragic hero is aware that there is no hope. But still, Camus concludes, he is happy! This short essay certainly is worth pondering upon, but I was a bit disappointed it was so short. </span>
  <a data-text-id="6731240461915250493" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="B" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/13826360-b"><img alt="B" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/607548742?book_show_action=true">May 05, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer607548742" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6171917240510514065">Had to give up on this book after 50 pages. I'm a big fan of existentialism and philosophy in general but this book left me completely unsatisfied. Besides a really important idea: that suicide is the only serious philosophical problem, I don't really think The Myth of Sisyphus has much to offer. It's either an extremely tough read or just plain incoherent babble. I'm inclined to say it is the latter. Overall, a huge disappointment.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1937623766" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1937623766" />
    <a title="Michael Finocchiaro" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/48483884-michael-finocchiaro"><img alt="Michael Finocchiaro" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1576692048p2/48483884.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1937623766?book_show_action=true">Mar 10, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12593786598402572442">More of an absurdist philosophical text than anything else, in Myth of Sisyphus, Camus draws for us a sketch of his existentialist ideas - those which underpinned his masterpieces such as the inestimable La Peste (The Plague).</span>

          </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1954431842" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1954431842" />
    <a title="Sebastien" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/259402-sebastien"><img alt="Sebastien" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1586709340p2/259402.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1954431842?book_show_action=true">May 13, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sebastien" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sebastien" href="/user/show/259402-sebastien">Sebastien</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9782070322886" href="/book/show/15714.Le_mythe_de_Sisyphe">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1954431842" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1061608958051730560">Just a couple quick notes that struck me:<br/><br/>There are some great essays here, two that I enjoyed were the ones on philosopher writers of the absurd who explored the struggle of the human condition: Dostoevsky and Kafka. It was great reading the Kafka essay because I just read The Trial a few days ago and I have wanted to see what kind of analysis is out there. Well, as luck has it I randomly stumbled upon Camus' take! There are worse people to get analysis from hehe, especially on the literature of</span>
  <span id="freeText1061608958051730560" style="display:none">Just a couple quick notes that struck me:<br /><br />There are some great essays here, two that I enjoyed were the ones on philosopher writers of the absurd who explored the struggle of the human condition: Dostoevsky and Kafka. It was great reading the Kafka essay because I just read The Trial a few days ago and I have wanted to see what kind of analysis is out there. Well, as luck has it I randomly stumbled upon Camus' take! There are worse people to get analysis from hehe, especially on the literature of the absurd. Much of what Camus said about this story mirrored some of my thinking which is always a bit of a relief to feel like hey at least I'm not completely missing the boat on this (of course he understands and explores the work with greater depth and nuance).<br /><br />Camus specifically says you need to reread the Kafka work (pay close attention to all the little details) and that there are many ways to interpret, multiple layers, multiple meanings, and it is all purposeful. He def holds Kafka and Dostoevsky in high regard for both their philosophy and their ability as artists. The Dostoevsky essay is interesting, I won't get into it now, I'm planning on reading Demons soon and excited for it. But if you are Dostoevsky fan you should check out the Camus essay and see what you think of his analysis.<br /><br />Another fun quirk, I was reading Vonnegut's Man From Another Country and he specifically referenced Camus' essay on suicide which I had just read. That was just a fun little serendipitous thing. I mean, in a way not surprising, because I have to imagine Vonnegut derived a lot of inspiration from Camus and the other writers who delved into the absurd and the struggle of the human condition. But still fun. <br /><br />In looking back I think I've only read The Stranger plus a few short stories by Camus. All a long time ago. As an adolescent I had this misperception that because the stories I read were so clear, seemingly simple, and plain easy to read that he and his philosophy were simple, stripped down, and not super deep. Given his influence on writers and thinkers I respect, I realized my superficial impressions were probably wrong and there was likely much more lurking beneath the surface. Reading these essays is like looking under the hood, you see the machinery behind Camus' thinking and stories. Why does he write about the themes he does and how deep does he go? Much more nuance and several layers of thinking and logic going on. Very cool. Def going to further explore his work! Plus the themes he explores appeal to me.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1061608958051730560" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('XJrJdk3z5v/YumZFi7zRetlr+8TWH3qYyxFkK4e5ISOMm9bf0AjyZZZH6ZuncM84SyUGT/glh026NSZxlhVcgw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

The unsta<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText17513559556035767432\" style=\"display:none\">It was to correct common misconceptions about his thought that Jean-Paul Sartre, the most dominent European intellectual of the post-World War II decades, accepted an invitation to speak on October 29, 1945, at the Club Maintenant in Paris. The unstated objective of his lecture (“Existentialism Is a Humanism”) was to expound his philosophy as a form of “existentialism,” a term much bandied about at the time. Sartre asserted that existentialism was essentially a doctrine for philosophers, though, ironically, he was about to make it accessible to a general audience. The published text of his lecture quickly became one of the bibles of existentialism and made Sartre an international celebrity.\n\nThe idea of freedom occupies the center of Sartre’s doctrine. Man, born into an empty, godless universe, is nothing to begin with. He creates his essence—his self, his being—through the choices he freely makes (“existence precedes essence”). Were it not for the contingency of his death, he would never end. Choosing to be this or that is to affirm the value of what we choose. In choosing, therefore, we commit not only ourselves but all of mankind.\n\nThis book presents a new English translation of Sartre’s 1945 lecture and his analysis of Camus’s The Stranger, along with a discussion of these works by acclaimed Sartre biographer Annie Cohen-Solal. This edition is a translation of the 1996 French edition, which includes Arlette Elkaïm-Sartre’s introduction and a Q&amp;A with Sartre about his lecture.<\/span>\n  <a With wit and energy, he turns from this critique to a philosophy that celebrates the present and demands that the individual imposes their own \'will to power\' upon the world.\n\nThis edition includes a commentary on the text by the translator and Michael Tanner\'s introduction, which explains some of the more abstract passages in Beyond Good and Evil.\n\nFrederich Nietzsche (1844-1900) became the chair of classical philology at Basel University at the age of 24 until his bad health forced him to retire in 1879. Lawrence, Thomas Mann and Jean-Paul Sartre, was considerable.\n\nIf you enjoyed Beyond Good and Evil you might like Nietzsche\'s Thus Spoke Zarathustra, also available in Penguin He is regarded as a leading pioneer of existentialism and one of the greatest philosophers of the 19th Century.\n\nIn Fear and Trembling, Kierkegaard wanted to understand the anxiety that must have been present in Abraham when God commanded him to offer his son as a human sacrifice. Abraham had a choice to complete the task or to forget it. He resigned himself to the loss of his son, acting according to his faith. In other words, one must be willing to give up all his or her earthly possessions in infinite resignation and must also be willing to give up whatever it is that he or she loves more than God. Nietzsche rewrites the former as a history of cruelty, exposing the central values of the Judaeo-Christian and liberal traditions - compassion, equality, justice - as the product of a brutal process of conditioning designed to domesticate the animal vitality of earlier cultures. The result is a book which raises profoundly disquieting issues about the violence of both ethics and interpretation. Nietzsche questions moral certainties by showing that religion and science have no claim to absolute truth, before turning on his own arguments in order to call their very presuppositions into question. The Genealogy is the most sustained of Nietzsche\'s later works and offers one of the fullest expressions of his characteristic concerns. This edition places his ideas within the cultural context of his own time and stresses the relevance of his work for a contemporary audience.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"3236821877842820022\" The majority of the book is a systematic attack upon the interpretations of Christ\'s words by St. Paul and those who followed him. Nietzsche claimed in the Foreword to have written the book for a very limited readership. In order to understand the book, he asserted that the reader &quot;... must be honest in intellectual matters to the point of hardness to so much as endure my seriousness, my passion.&quot; The reader should be above politics and nationalism.
